The ‘machos’ in her photographs are seen performing their dominant roles within the patriarchal system of Latin America, but in fact, Voleau is the one controlling these relationships’ dynamics, by constantly redirecting the narrative and dismantling how they perform their masculinities. As she fluidly transitions from iPhone snapshots to classic film portraiture, from selfies to GoPro underwater shots, one can’t help but wonder about the storyline behind her portraits. With Hola Mi Amol Voleau explores the power balance between subject and object, while challenging the viewers’ presumptions of the roles played by the different - Chapter 10 What should we believe in the face of panic_3.jpg","contentType":"image/jpeg"}}}],"description":"Gao Shang presents his work Honesty and Disguise as panels, many of which are panoramic in format, in which photographs are connected and overlap. They are laid out as if on a digital surface; some are drawn like a network diagram. There are recurring motifs of fire, disappearing figures and guns held up as if aiming at a training range. Language and the image are used to evoke and inform, confuse and contradict. The work calls on us to read both through and around the image, to see not just its apparent content, but also the conditions of its making. As we search for meaning, it becomes clear that Shang organises for us the crumbs of a possible narrative. Shang himself suggests this imagery is like a photographer’s novel and might be akin to the reading of a dream, a kind of image analysis that requires oblique strategies. 2014, Alba Zari discovered that the man she had always considered to be her father, in fact was not: a DNA test showed that the probability of paternity was 0%. Since the Y chromosome can only be passed on from father to son, The Y represents a fascinating collection of material that Zari gathered over the course of two years while searching for information that otherwise would have been easily acquired if she had been born a man. It accumulates fuzzy screengrabs from VHS home movies; manipulated snapshots from her family albums; physiognomic headshots of Zari, her brother, and of men she had thought to be her father throughout the search. Zari even acquired 3D scans of her own head, creating a computer-generated avatar of herself as an emotionally ‘neutral’ 50-year-old man, in order to image-search the internet for a man who might visually match. Ultimately, The Y questions photography as a document of proof. - Worlds Within Worlds_Guanyu Xu.jpg","contentType":"image/jpeg"}}}],"description":"In Temporarily Censored Home, Xu covertly situated photographs in his teenage home in Beijing to queer the normativity of his parents' heterosexual space. These images taken in the past four years consist of portraits of himself and other gay men in their domestic settings; prints of his artworks made in the US; photographs of landscapes and built environments taken in the United States, Europe, and China; torn pages from film and fashion magazines that he collected as a teenager; and images from his family photo albums. Through positioning and layering images, he aims to juxtapose, contradict, and collapse space and time, disrupting his teenage home. It bridges the relationship between the personal and political in the context of the oppressive systems of both China and the US. Even though he would set up and take down these installations before his parents would return, Xu temporarily reclaimed his home in Beijing as a queer space of freedom and temporary cube 30","file":{"url":"//images.ctfassets.net/x5vaujfoja7e/1PxDYYLJiS5hQPs7oRzCm2/7c75ef9cd22636307399a3416cc1719b/maggic_cube_30.jpg","details":{"size":2766659,"image":{"width":1476,"height":2021}},"fileName":"maggic_cube_30.jpg","contentType":"image/jpeg"}}}],"description":"With her series Maggic Cube, Adji Dieye uses the well-known Maggi stock cube as a metaphor to reveal the impact of global imperialistic trade on West African contemporary cultural identities. Dieye attempts to dissect and reinterpret the branding and communication strategies used by brands such as the Swiss Maggi and its Spanish equivalent Jumbo. Her research is focused on its implications in a Senegalese context, where the Maggi cube has slowly replaced common local ingredients, and its branding perpetuates stereotypical feminine roles within Senegalese society. Dieye’s female subjects appear faceless or masked, by means of subverting and underlining the cultural and gendered constructs created with these companies’ advertisements. The series takes its aesthetics from the works of masters of photography, such as Malick Sidibé and Seydou Keïta, questioning the contemporary art economy’s tendency to reduce specific visual styles, in this case from West African countries, into representative formats for an entire 2010, Camillo Pasquarelli travelled to Kashmir – a disputed territory claimed by India, Pakistan and China since the Indo-Pakistani War of 1947-1948. Struck by both the poetic beauty of the land and the tragic dimension of the regional conflict, Pasquarelli started to study the Kashmiri people and their struggle for independence. After having conducted more photojournalistic projects on the region, Monsoons Never Cross the Mountains brings in a more emotional view upon life within the conflict-torn valley through the eyes of its children. Pasquarelli’s photographs reference the memories and daily realities of a stolen childhood: a strange existence that is never fully understood by the children who experience it. The series is named after a poem by the Kashmiri-American poet Agha Shahid Ali that describes the distinctive climate of Kashmir, which — due to its surrounding mountains — is quite different from the to her promiscuity and passion. After being sacrificed to the gods, she is reincarnated as a red lotus. In this series, montage is used as a technique to imitate the collage-like character of folk tales. Sukchai is interested in the ideologies that shape society’s use of the allegorical tales handed down to us, and how stories from the past can be manipulated to control us in the present. In these narratives, a woman displaying her sexuality is often presented as a form of transgression. Reconsidering the references to her own name, Kamonlak, which is Thai for ‘lotus’ , with this retelling of the age-old tale, Sukchai explores how myths can be retold in order to oppose the ideologies they have been made to Montgomery’s Flash Points is a portrait of American volatility—a view of a nation in flux, torn between its future and its past. The Ferguson unrest, in 2014, served as a flashpoint not only for the photographer’s understanding of his home country but for America itself. The sense of optimism and progress that had been reflected in the election of the first black president had given way to something else—a fractured, churning, conflicted state. The subjects of Montgomery’s images are everyday Americans caught between moments of grief, crisis, anger, euphoria, fear, and relief. While working in the tradition of documentary photography, Montgomery’s approach is markedly more cinematic, capturing in sharp relief a nation frozen like a film still—a tragedy unfolding without end."}},{"sys":{"space":{"sys":{"type":"Link","linkType":"Space","id":"x5vaujfoja7e"}},"id":"51JPANeK7OGR3D1bEOWu4Q","type":"Entry","createdAt":"2020-11-11T10:53:58.677Z","updatedAt":"2020-11-11T10:53:58.677Z","environment":{"sys":{"id":"master","type":"Link","linkType":"Environment"}},"revision":1,"contentType":{"sys":{"type":"Link","linkType":"ContentType","id":"project"}},"locale":"en-US"},"fields":{"name":"PVC October 2018, Aàdesokan began to actively observe the notorious 'meat highway' in Lagos: a chaotic roadside strip of unregulated butchers' stalls selling meat under less than salubrious conditions. His observations evolved into a collaboration with rising fashion designer Sanusi Lagos, fusing functionality and fashion by dressing the Lagos butchers in PVC-made outfits, making them stand out while maintaining a decent level of health standard. The sheer absurdity of dressing the 'crass butchers' in high fashion draws upon a universally accepted luxury good to elevate an ill-treated one, questioning our notions of luxury and display. While the work seems to comically hover somewhere in between fashion photography and a public health campaign, surreal splashes – sometimes of blood, sometimes of negative spots within the photograph – mark the image as merely a surface. Like the PVC hygienic protection itself, the photograph becomes a screen barrier to be considered by those visually consuming the work. 1 copy.jpg","contentType":"image/jpeg"}}}],"description":"Through a mixed approach of piecing together, layering, and re-photographing, Figure as Index quite literally holds together a community — Luther Konadu’s close friends. Even when subjects appear ostensibly alone, they are multiplied, their bodies presented in a staggered progression or with intentional misregistration. The people in Luther Konadu’s pictures are all black and brown, but their blackness and brownness is so clearly varied and subject to the idiosyncrasies of printers and cameras. Viewers looking closely will find diasporas within diasporas, a collage just like the compositions themselves. Some portraits are taken in the mirror: the camera ultimately photographing its own reflection, showing the viewer the act of being photographed. Figure as Index opens up the question of whether photographs can be correctives for photography’s dark histories, and if we ever really understand what it means to be an attempt to minimise the devastating effect of global warming on the glaciers of the Swiss Alps, each summer they are covered with geotextile blankets that are designed to keep the ice cool. Douglas Mandry journeyed his native country, intrigued by this desperate attempt to prevent the receding glaciers from further melting. The artist collected disused pieces of geotextile from the mountains, and later processed them as a base for found photographs of early twentieth-century alpinism in the region. Mandry also took bits of broken ice from the glacier with him in a cool box. In the darkroom, he put the ice in the enlarger, where it started to melt and drip onto the photo paper, creating a colourful aura of the glacier. Mandry then reproduced these photograms on a glass plate. Monuments could be considered an index of the vanishing glaciers of Switzerland: frozen time, captured in an instant by the principles of photography."}},{"sys":{"space":{"sys":{"type":"Link","linkType":"Space","id":"x5vaujfoja7e"}},"id":"cn2BOBCxFbWoXmDahETYM","type":"Entry","createdAt":"2020-11-11T10:54:04.971Z","updatedAt":"2020-11-11T10:54:04.971Z","environment":{"sys":{"id":"master","type":"Link","linkType":"Environment"}},"revision":1,"contentType":{"sys":{"type":"Link","linkType":"ContentType","id":"project"}},"locale":"en-US"},"fields":{"name":"To In Matthew Leifheit’s feverish depictions, we are lured into an intergenerational tableau of naked men in the night. They adorn the interior of a baroque 1957 sex hotel called the Belvedere, or are seen close up as reclining Caravaggio-esque beauties on the beach, their skin glistening with sweat. Operatic in tone, the series contains both libidinous beauty and morbid tragedy.  But Leifheit does not singularly capture the contemporary queer culture of the island. This series also functions as a palimpsest of many nights and many histories, searching for lost role models such as artist Paul Thek, whose 1969 sculpture \"Fishman\" is referenced in a portrait. A narrow strip of sand withheld in place by a fragile dune ecosystem, the island is in imminent danger of destruction by rising sea levels as an effect of climate Serraf was born and raised in Zimbabwe and fled to South Africa where he is currently based. In the mobility that has been a defining factor of his life, Micha has taken note of the behaviour of other foreign nationals in their navigation of post-apartheid South Africa. They display an acute awareness of the safest shape to take on in order to survive particular contexts. This fluid presentation of self, and the ability to be malleable are tactics used to access acceptance and camouflage. In this exposure to several ways of existing, Micha has experienced and observed a variety of gender norms, enactments and ideologies and seeks to dissect and dismantle the understanding of them by demonstrating the evolutionary, fluid and emotional entanglements related to the purpose, interpretations and performance of gender. *Micha Serraf choses to shift between personal pronouns to mark the fluidity of gender is one of the world’s fastest growing economies, with a thriving consumerist society —despite being one of the last remaining communist governments in power. Fascinated by this friction, and its roots in the infamous Vietnam War, Simone Sapienza embarked on an ambitious project to go beyond the iconic representations of Vietnam and capture these contemporary contradictions. After a period of research, he spent a number of years developing a visual language to portray the cultural, political and social constructs of this nation. Many of the images lie on the border between fiction and reality, revealing the artifice of the media and the communist government’s theatrical displays of history and power. Sapienza questions not only how Western societies consider the country based on popular culture and mythology in the post-Vietnam War decades, but also critically examines the notion of truthfulness in the tradition of documentary photography itself. is a Hausa word that translates to ‘tales’ – folktales or fairytales for children. In this spirit, Gambo created her project as a poetic alternative narrative about the experiences of students schooling in Northeastern Nigeria, where the Shehu Sanda Kyarimi Government Secondary School was attacked by Boko Haram terrorists in 2013. The artist first visited the school in 2017 to interact with a group of final year students when it had just been reopened after a two-year closure following the abduction of the Chibok girls. She executed a performative workshop with the students in order to plot an area for playful interaction where the undefined can be stimulated without a direct imposition of meaning. This intent was inspired by Trinh T. Minh-ha's film \"Reassemblage\" (1983), a montage of fleeting images from Senegal without narration, in which Trinh explains that she intends ‘not to speak about/Just speak nearby,’ unlike more conventional ethnographic documentary Yatromanolakis 22","file":{"url":"//images.ctfassets.net/x5vaujfoja7e/7Q0rEy1PzZ0O35CUnLRe7/69832b8eed296e5c8e72cdc04b930b0b/yatromanolakis_24.jpg","details":{"size":1099824,"image":{"width":1350,"height":900}},"fileName":"yatromanolakis_24.jpg","contentType":"image/jpeg"}}}],"description":"This series by Yorgos Yatromanolakis was made in reaction to a period of intense introspection, sparked by a series of traumatic events in his personal life. Ending up back home in Crete, he found comfort in nature and the idea that, like a caterpillar in a cocoon, he might emerge transformed. This dark time is reflected in his images, which were mostly taken at twilight and etched out in inky blues and blacks. However they are also shot through with light, suggesting transition rather than total blackness. Within the narrative of the series, this experience is intertwined with the biological processes of transformation in nature, which are taking place in parallel and with which the photographer coexists and identifies. While the project was first published as a book and exhibited as framed photographs, Foam Talent 2020 is the very first occasion in which Yatromanolakis has transformed his work into an immersive audiovisual installation. Hamun is connected to Helmand/Hirmand River which begins in Afghanistan. The Afghan government has built extensive dams upstream from Helmand, preventing its water from reaching the lake, located in the Iranian province of Sistan and Baluchestan. It has been suffering from drought for the last 18 years, which has turned a region once famed for its agriculture and forests into a vast, infertile desert. Hashem Shakeri has travelled from north to south of this province to document the water shortage and drought, and the subsequent famine, unemployment and depopulation it has caused. By shooting three to four exposures for each frame of his analogue medium format camera to deal with the sharp sunlight, all depth and contrast, like the water of Hamun, is drained from the image. What is left is the absolute silence of the region, and its people who seem to live in continuous grief."}},{"sys":{"space":{"sys":{"type":"Link","linkType":"Space","id":"x5vaujfoja7e"}},"id":"3bfhZJCxmgYRgoJ37jVwSp","type":"Entry","createdAt":"2020-11-11T10:54:00.583Z","updatedAt":"2020-11-11T10:54:00.583Z","environment":{"sys":{"id":"master","type":"Link","linkType":"Environment"}},"revision":1,"contentType":{"sys":{"type":"Link","linkType":"ContentType","id":"project"}},"locale":"en-US"},"fields":{"name":"Escape from Paradise.jpg","contentType":"image/jpeg"}}}],"description":"The Hawaiian shirt is in fact a multi-ethnic composition made with a Western shape, of Japanese fabric, by Chinese tailors, in a Filipino style – the literal embodiment of an exotic dream. This realisation led artist Benoît Jeannet to research the representation of the Hawaiian myth. He started collecting landscape posters and photographing their glossy surface with a direct flash, creating a cloudy effect that evokes another well-known image engraved in collective memory: of atomic tests in the Pacific. Where one island or atoll would be designated as a tropical holiday destination, another would be sacrificed to the world’s arms race. As such, photographs of exotic flowers suddenly resemble mushroom clouds; fossilised pineapples become silent reminders of Hawaii’s loss of independence to the fruit and sugar trade. The Hawaiian shirt itself originates from the ‘palaka’, a piece of clothing imported for plantation workers at the turn of the 20th century – it wasn’t exactly invented as the ultimate holiday outfit. These objects and images are juxtaposed with pictures from the National Archives and Records Administration (NARA) on the atomic tests in the Pacific. Escape From Paradise brings together signs of capitalism, colonialism, consumerism, warfare and environmental disaster that hide behind the popular representation of paradise. Primeira Imagem.jpg","contentType":"image/jpeg"}}}],"description":"Sofia Borges is a conceptual artist who uses the photographic medium to study philosophical notions around the relationship between matter and meaning. She is interested in the myths that inform our collective perception of reality through objects of artistic, cultural, historical, or scientific importance. Using collage, performance, fire and layers of pigments, Borges creates images that are so strange that they often get mistaken for digital manipulations. The radically odd aesthetic of her photographs, however, comes from the fact that she builds complex photographic scenarios that are either born from something that exists or from something that is constructed and then photographed. There are seemingly unanswerable questions imbedded in Borges’s work: What is the connection between matter and meaning? What is photography? What is an image? She is not looking to provide answers but rather to show us how impossible it is to understand the material world through representation.
